<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Gastroenteritis</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Inflammation of the stomach and small intestine</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="background:#ccc">Gastroenteritis</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Other names</th><td class="infobox-data">Gastro, stomach bug, stomach virus, stomach flu, gastric flu, gastrointestinitis</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gastroenteritis_viruses.jpg"><img alt="Gastroenteritis viruses.jpg" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="419" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Gastroenteritis_viruses.jpg/300px-Gastroenteritis_viruses.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Gastroenteritis_viruses.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2">Gastroenteritis viruses: A = rotavirus, B = adenovirus, C = norovirus and D = astrovirus. <a href="/wiki/Gastroenterology" title="Gastroenterology">gastroenterology</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms" title="Signs and symptoms">Symptoms</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">Diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">abdominal</a> pain, <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">Complications</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">Dehydration</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fer2015_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fer2015-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Causes</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">Viruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helm2006_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Helm2006-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">Diagnostic method</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Based on symptoms, occasionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stool_culture" title="Stool culture">stool culture</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Inflammatory_bowel_disease" title="Inflammatory bowel disease">Inflammatory bowel disease</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" fluids</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">2.4 billion (2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015Pre_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015Pre-6">[6]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Deaths</th><td class="infobox-data">1.3 million (2015)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-7">[7]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Gastroenteritis</b>, also known as <b>infectious diarrhea</b> and <b>gastro</b>, is <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">gastrointestinal tract</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestine" title="Intestine">intestine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch2015-8">[8]</a></sup> Symptoms may include <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">Fever</a>, lack of energy, and <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> may also occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fer2015_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fer2015-3">[3]</a></sup> This typically lasts less than two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch2015_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sch2015-8">[8]</a></sup> It is not related to <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, even though in the U.S. it is sometimes called the "<b>stomach flu</b>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gastroenteritis is usually caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">viruses</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helm2006_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Helm2006-4">[4]</a></sup> gut <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a> can also cause gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helm2006_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Helm2006-4">[4]</a></sup> In children, <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">rotavirus</a> is the most common cause of severe disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota2012_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rota2012-10">[10]</a></sup> In adults, <a href="/wiki/Norovirus" title="Norovirus">norovirus</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">Campylobacter</a></i> are common causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21695033_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21695033-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22025030_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22025030-12">[12]</a></sup> Eating improperly prepared food, drinking contaminated water or close contact with a person who is infected can <a href="#Transmission">spread the disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> Treatment is generally the same with or without a definitive diagnosis, so testing to confirm is usually not needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>For young children in impoverished countries, prevention includes <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> with soap, drinking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clean_water" title="Clean water">clean water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> babies instead of using <a href="/wiki/Infant_formula" title="Infant formula">formula</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> and proper disposal of <a href="/wiki/Human_waste" title="Human waste">human waste</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus_vaccine" title="Rotavirus vaccine">rotavirus vaccine</a> is recommended as a prevention for children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota2012_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rota2012-10">[10]</a></sup> Treatment involves getting enough fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> For mild or moderate cases, this can typically be achieved by drinking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_solution" title="Oral rehydration solution">oral rehydration solution</a> (a combination of water, salts and sugar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> In those who are breastfed, continued breastfeeding is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> For more severe cases, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intravenous_fluids" title="Intravenous fluids">intravenous fluids</a> may be needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-15"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> Fluids may also be given by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasogastric_tube" title="Nasogastric tube">nasogastric tube</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zinc</a> supplementation is recommended in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ci2013_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-Ci2013-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">Antibiotics</a> are generally not needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> However, antibiotics are recommended for young children with a fever and bloody diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, there were two billion cases of gastroenteritis, resulting in 1.3 million deaths globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015Pre_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015Pre-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-7">[7]</a></sup> Children and those in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> are affected the most.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> In 2011, there were about 1.7 billion cases, resulting in about 700,000 deaths of children under the age of five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walk2013_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walk2013-16">[16]</a></sup> In the developing world, children less than two years of age frequently get six or more infections a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> It is less common in adults, partly due to the development of <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Management"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Management</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BristolStoolChart.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="698" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/BristolStoolChart.png/370px-BristolStoolChart.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/BristolStoolChart.png/555px-BristolStoolChart.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9e/BristolStoolChart.png 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BristolStoolChart.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bristol_stool_scale" title="Bristol stool scale">Bristol stool chart</a></div></div></div>
<p>Gastroenteritis usually involves both <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> Sometimes, only one or the other is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> This may be accompanied by abdominal cramps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Signs and symptoms usually begin 12–72 hours after contracting the infectious agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> If due to a virus, the condition usually resolves within one week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> Some viral infections also involve <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, fatigue, <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> If the <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">stool is bloody</a>, the cause is less likely to be viral<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> and more likely to be bacterial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup> Some bacterial infections cause severe abdominal pain and may persist for several weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Children infected with rotavirus usually make a full recovery within three to eight days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota2011_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rota2011-20">[20]</a></sup> However, in poor countries treatment for severe infections is often out of reach and persistent diarrhea is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">Dehydration</a> is a common complication of <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2009_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2009-22">[22]</a></sup> Severe dehydration in children may be recognized if the skin color and position returns slowly when pressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> This is called "prolonged <a href="/wiki/Capillary_refill" title="Capillary refill">capillary refill</a>" and "poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_turgor" title="Skin turgor">skin turgor</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> Abnormal breathing is another sign of severe dehydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> Repeat infections are typically seen in areas with poor sanitation, and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stunted_growth" title="Stunted growth">Stunted growth</a> and long-term cognitive delays can result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reactive_arthritis" title="Reactive arthritis">Reactive arthritis</a> occurs in 1% of people following infections with <i>Campylobacter</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a> occurs in 0.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemolytic_uremic_syndrome" title="Hemolytic uremic syndrome">Hemolytic uremic syndrome</a> (HUS) may occur due to infection with <a href="/wiki/Shiga_toxin" title="Shiga toxin">Shiga toxin</a>-producing <i>Escherichia coli</i> or <i>Shigella</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2007_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2007-24">[24]</a></sup> HUS causes <a href="/wiki/Thrombocytopenia" title="Thrombocytopenia">low platelet counts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renal_failure" title="Renal failure">poor kidney function</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hemolytic_anemia" title="Hemolytic anemia">low red blood cell count (due to their breakdown)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2007_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2007-24">[24]</a></sup> Children are more predisposed to getting HUS than adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Some viral infections may produce <a href="/wiki/Benign_infantile_epilepsy" title="Benign infantile epilepsy">benign infantile seizures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cause">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">Viruses</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">rotavirus</a> (in children) and <a href="/wiki/Norovirus" title="Norovirus">norovirus</a> (in adults)) and the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">Campylobacter</a></i> species are the primary causes of gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sz2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sz2010-25">[25]</a></sup> There are, however, many other infectious agents that can cause this syndrome including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helm2006_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Helm2006-4">[4]</a></sup> Non-infectious causes are seen on occasion, but they are less likely than a viral or bacterial cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Risk of infection is higher in children due to their lack of <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Children are also at higher risk because they are less likely to practice good <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> habits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Children living in areas without easy <a href="/wiki/WASH" title="WASH">access to water and soap</a> are especially vulnerable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral">Viral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Viral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rotavirus" title="Rotavirus">Rotaviruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norovirus" title="Norovirus">noroviruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adenovirus" title="Adenovirus">adenoviruses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astrovirus" title="Astrovirus">astroviruses</a> are known to cause viral gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumar2020_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar2020-26">[26]</a></sup> Rotavirus is the most common cause of gastroenteritis in children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sz2010_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sz2010-25">[25]</a></sup> and produces similar rates in both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">developed</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota2011_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rota2011-20">[20]</a></sup> Viruses cause about 70% of episodes of infectious diarrhea in the pediatric age group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> Rotavirus is a less common cause in adults due to acquired immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ID2011_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ID2011-27">[27]</a></sup> Norovirus is the cause in about 18% of all cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Generally speaking, viral gastroenteritis accounts for 21–40% of the cases of infectious diarrhea in developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Norovirus is the leading cause of gastroenteritis among adults in America accounting for about 90% of viral gastroenteritis outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> These localized <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemics</a> typically occur when groups of people spend time proximate to each other, such as on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cruise_ships" title="Cruise ships">cruise ships</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> in hospitals, or in restaurants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> People may remain infectious even after their diarrhea has ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> Norovirus is the cause of about 10% of cases in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial">Bacterial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Bacterial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg/220px-Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg/330px-Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg/440px-Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salmonella_Typhimurium_Gram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Salmonella enterica</i> serovar Typhimurium (ATCC 14028) as seen with a microscope at 1000 fold magnification and following Gram staining</div></div></div>
<p>In some countries, <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter_jejuni" title="Campylobacter jejuni">Campylobacter jejuni</a></i> is the primary cause of bacterial gastroenteritis, with half of these cases associated with exposure to <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup> In children, bacteria are the cause in about 15% of cases, with the most common types being <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shigella" title="Shigella">Shigella</a></i>, and <i>Campylobacter</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> If food becomes contaminated with bacteria and remains at room temperature for a period of several hours, the bacteria multiply and increase the risk of infection in those who consume the food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Some foods commonly associated with illness include raw or undercooked meat, poultry, seafood, and eggs; raw sprouts; unpasteurized milk and soft cheeses; and fruit and vegetable juices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In the developing world, especially sub-Saharan Africa and Asia, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> is a common cause of gastroenteritis. This infection is usually transmitted by contaminated water or food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cholera11_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cholera11-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Toxigenic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_colitis" title="Clostridium difficile colitis">Clostridium difficile</a></i> is an important cause of diarrhea that occurs more often in the elderly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Infants can carry these bacteria without developing symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> It is a common cause of diarrhea in those who are hospitalized and is frequently associated with antibiotic use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> infectious diarrhea may also occur in those who have used antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Acute "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traveler%27s_diarrhea" title="Traveler's diarrhea">traveler's diarrhea</a>" is usually a type of bacterial gastroenteritis, while the persistent form is usually parasitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Travelers'_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Travelers'-34">[34]</a></sup> Acid-suppressing medication appears to increase the risk of significant infection after exposure to a number of organisms, including <i>Clostridium difficile</i>, <i>Salmonella</i>, and <i>Campylobacter</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PPI2007_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-PPI2007-35">[35]</a></sup> The risk is greater in those taking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_pump_inhibitors" title="Proton pump inhibitors">proton pump inhibitors</a> than with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H2_antagonist" title="H2 antagonist">H<sub>2</sub> antagonists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PPI2007_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-PPI2007-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitic">Parasitic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Parasitic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasites" title="Parasites">parasites</a> can cause gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giardia_lamblia" title="Giardia lamblia">Giardia lamblia</a></i> is most common, but <i><a href="/wiki/Entamoeba_histolytica" title="Entamoeba histolytica">Entamoeba histolytica</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptosporidium" title="Cryptosporidium">Cryptosporidium</a></i> spp., and other species have also been implicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Travelers'_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Travelers'-34">[34]</a></sup> As a group, these agents comprise about 10% of cases in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2007_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2007-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Travelers'_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Travelers'-34">[34]</a></sup> <i>Giardia</i> occurs more commonly in the developing world, but this type of illness can occur nearly everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giar2010_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giar2010-36">[36]</a></sup> It occurs more commonly in persons who have traveled to areas with high prevalence, children who attend <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_care" title="Day care">day care</a>, <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">men who have sex with men</a>, and following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disasters" title="Disasters">disasters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giar2010_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giar2010-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Transmission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Transmission may occur from drinking contaminated water or when people share personal objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> Water quality typically worsens during the rainy season and outbreaks are more common at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperateness" title="Temperateness">areas with four seasons</a>, infections are more common in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Worldwide, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bottle-feeding" title="Bottle-feeding">bottle-feeding</a> of babies with improperly sanitized bottles is a significant cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> Transmission rates are also related to poor hygiene, (especially among children),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> in crowded households,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and in those with poor nutritional status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Adults who have developed immunities might still carry certain organisms without exhibiting symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Thus, adults can become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_reservoirs" title="Natural reservoirs">natural reservoirs</a> of certain diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> While some agents (such as <i>Shigella</i>) only occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primates" title="Primates">primates</a>, others (such as <i>Giardia</i>) may occur in a wide variety of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-11"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-infectious">Non-infectious</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Non-infectious">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are a number of non-infectious causes of inflammation of the gastrointestinal tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Some of the more common include medications (like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAIDs" title="NSAIDs">NSAIDs</a>), certain foods such as <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> (in those who are intolerant), and <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> (in those with <a href="/wiki/Coeliac_disease" title="Coeliac disease">celiac disease</a>). <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn's disease">Crohn's disease</a> is also a non-infectious source of (often severe) gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Disease secondary to <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> may also occur. Some food-related conditions associated with nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea include: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ciguatera" title="Ciguatera">ciguatera poisoning</a> due to consumption of contaminated predatory fish, <a href="/wiki/Scombroid_food_poisoning" title="Scombroid food poisoning">scombroid</a> associated with the consumption of certain types of spoiled fish, <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin#Poisoning" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin poisoning</a> from the consumption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puffer_fish" title="Puffer fish">puffer fish</a> among others, and <a href="/wiki/Botulism" title="Botulism">botulism</a> typically due to improperly preserved food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, rates of emergency department use for noninfectious gastroenteritis dropped 30% from 2006 until 2011. Of the twenty most common conditions seen in the emergency department, rates of noninfectious gastroenteritis had the largest decrease in visits in that time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gastroenteritis is defined as <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a> due to inflammation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_bowel" title="Small bowel">small</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large_bowel" title="Large bowel">large bowel</a>, often due to infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-12"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> The changes in the small bowel are typically noninflammatory, while the ones in the large bowel are inflammatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-13"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> The number of pathogens required to cause an infection varies from as few as one (for <i>Cryptosporidium</i>) to as many as 10<sup>8</sup> (for <i>Vibrio cholerae</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-14"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gastroenteritis is typically diagnosed clinically, based on a person's signs and symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> Determining the exact cause is usually not needed as it does not alter the management of the condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stool_culture" title="Stool culture">stool cultures</a> should be performed in those with blood in the stool, those who might have been exposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a>, and those who have recently traveled to the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> It may also be appropriate in children younger than 5, old people, and those with poor immune function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IDSA2017_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-IDSA2017-40">[40]</a></sup> Diagnostic testing may also be done for surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">hypoglycemia</a> occurs in approximately 10% of infants and young children, measuring serum <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> in this population is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrolytes" title="Electrolytes">Electrolytes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creatinine" title="Creatinine">kidney function</a> should also be checked when there is a concern about severe dehydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dehydration">Dehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A determination of whether or not the person has <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> is an important part of the assessment, with dehydration typically divided into mild (3–5%), moderate (6–9%), and severe (≥10%) cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> In children, the most accurate signs of moderate or severe dehydration are a prolonged <a href="/wiki/Capillary_refill" title="Capillary refill">capillary refill</a>, poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_turgor" title="Skin turgor">skin turgor</a>, and abnormal breathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Other useful findings (when used in combination) include sunken eyes, decreased activity, a lack of tears, and a dry mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> A normal urinary output and oral fluid intake is reassuring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> Laboratory testing is of little clinical benefit in determining the degree of dehydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Thus the use of urine testing or ultrasounds is generally not needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Differential diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other potential causes of signs and symptoms that mimic those seen in gastroenteritis that need to be ruled out include <a href="/wiki/Appendicitis" title="Appendicitis">appendicitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volvulus" title="Volvulus">volvulus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inflammatory_bowel_disease" title="Inflammatory bowel disease">inflammatory bowel disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infection" title="Urinary tract infection">urinary tract infections</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes mellitus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pancreatic_insufficiency" title="Pancreatic insufficiency">Pancreatic insufficiency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Short_bowel_syndrome" title="Short bowel syndrome">short bowel syndrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whipple%27s_disease" title="Whipple's disease">Whipple's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coeliac_disease" title="Coeliac disease">coeliac disease</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Laxative" title="Laxative">laxative</a> abuse should also be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-43">[43]</a></sup> The differential diagnosis can be complicated somewhat if the person exhibits <i>only</i> vomiting or diarrhea (rather than both).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Appendicitis may present with vomiting, abdominal pain, and a small amount of diarrhea in up to 33% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-18"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> This is in contrast to the large amount of diarrhea that is typical of gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-19"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lower_respiratory_tract_infection" title="Lower respiratory tract infection">Infections of the lungs</a> or urinary tract in children may also cause vomiting or diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-20"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Classical <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_ketoacidosis" title="Diabetic ketoacidosis">diabetic ketoacidosis</a> (DKA) presents with abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting, but without diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-21"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> One study found that 17% of children with DKA were initially diagnosed as having gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-22"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RotavirusV2009.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="641" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RotavirusV2009.gif/290px-RotavirusV2009.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RotavirusV2009.gif/435px-RotavirusV2009.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/RotavirusV2009.gif/580px-RotavirusV2009.gif 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RotavirusV2009.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of rotavirus tests with positive results, by surveillance week, United States, July 2000 – June 2009</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Water.2C_sanitation.2C_hygiene"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Water,_sanitation,_hygiene">Water, sanitation, hygiene</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Water, sanitation, hygiene">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/WASH#Health_aspects" title="WASH">WASH § Health aspects</a></div>
<p>A supply of easily accessible uncontaminated water and good <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> practices are important for reducing rates of infection and clinically significant gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-15"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> Personal <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> measures (such as <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> with soap) have been found to decrease rates of gastroenteritis in both the developing and developed world by as much as 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> Alcohol-based gels may also be effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint10_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-Tint10-23">[23]</a></sup> Food or drink that is thought to be contaminated should be avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">Breastfeeding</a> is important, especially in places with poor hygiene, as is improvement of hygiene generally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> Breast milk reduces both the frequency of infections and their duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-23"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">Vaccination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vaccination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to both its effectiveness and safety, in 2009 the World Health Organization recommended that the <a href="/wiki/Rotavirus_vaccine" title="Rotavirus vaccine">rotavirus vaccine</a> be offered to all children globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sz2010_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sz2010-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHORota2009_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHORota2009-45">[45]</a></sup> Two commercial rotavirus vaccines exist and several more are in development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHORota2009_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHORota2009-45">[45]</a></sup> In Africa and Asia these vaccines reduced severe disease among infants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHORota2009_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHORota2009-45">[45]</a></sup> and countries that have put in place national immunization programs have seen a decline in the rates and severity of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> This vaccine may also prevent illness in non-vaccinated children by reducing the number of circulating infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Since 2000, the implementation of a rotavirus vaccination program in the United States has substantially decreased the number of cases of diarrhea by as much as 80 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rota_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rota-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The first dose of vaccine should be given to infants between 6 and 15 weeks of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sz2010_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sz2010-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cholera_vaccine" title="Cholera vaccine">oral cholera vaccine</a> has been found to be 50–60% effective over two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a number of vaccines against gastroenteritis in development. For example, vaccines against <i>Shigella</i> and enterotoxigenic <i>Escherichia coli</i> (ETEC), which are two of the leading bacterial causes of gastroenteritis worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_ETEC_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_ETEC-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Shig_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Shig-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gastroenteritis is usually an acute and self-limiting disease that does not require medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2009_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2009-22">[22]</a></sup> The preferred treatment in those with mild to moderate <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> is <a href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_therapy" title="Oral rehydration therapy">oral rehydration therapy</a> (ORT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2007_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2007-24">[24]</a></sup> For children at risk of dehydration from vomiting, taking a single dose of the <a href="/wiki/Antiemetic" title="Antiemetic">anti vomiting</a> medication <a href="/wiki/Metoclopramide" title="Metoclopramide">metoclopramide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ondansetron" title="Ondansetron">ondansetron</a>, may be helpful,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedorowicz2011_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fedorowicz2011-55">[55]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butylscopolamine" title="Butylscopolamine">butylscopolamine</a> is useful in treating <a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rehydration">Rehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary treatment of gastroenteritis in both children and adults is <a href="/wiki/Fluid_replacement" title="Fluid replacement">rehydration</a>. This is preferably achieved by drinking rehydration solution, although <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenous</a> delivery may be required if there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decreased_level_of_consciousness" title="Decreased level of consciousness">decreased level of consciousness</a> or if dehydration is severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Drinking replacement therapy products made with complex carbohydrates (i.e. those made from wheat or rice) may be superior to those based on simple sugars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Drinks especially high in simple sugars, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_drinks" title="Soft drinks">soft drinks</a> and fruit juices, are not recommended in children under five years of age as they may <i>increase</i> diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2009_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2009-22">[22]</a></sup> Plain water may be used if more specific ORT preparations are unavailable or the person is not willing to drink them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2009_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2009-22">[22]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasogastric_tube" title="Nasogastric tube">nasogastric tube</a> can be used in young children to administer fluids if warranted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2005_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2005-13">[13]</a></sup> In those who require intravenous fluids, one to four hours' worth is often sufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary">Dietary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dietary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is recommended that breast-fed infants continue to be nursed in the usual fashion,  and that formula-fed infants continue their formula immediately after rehydration with ORT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR2003_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR2003-61">[61]</a></sup> Lactose-free or lactose-reduced formulas usually are not necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR2003_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR2003-61">[61]</a></sup> Children should continue their usual diet during episodes of diarrhea with the exception that foods high in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_sugar" title="Simple sugar">simple sugars</a> should be avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR2003_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR2003-61">[61]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BRAT_diet" title="BRAT diet">BRAT diet</a> (bananas, rice, applesauce, toast and tea) is no longer recommended, as it contains insufficient nutrients and has no benefit over normal feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMWR2003_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-MMWR2003-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Cochrane Review from 2020 concludes that <a href="/wiki/Probiotic" title="Probiotic">probiotics</a> make little or no difference to people who have diarrhea lasting 2 days or longer and that there is no proof that they reduce its duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> They may be useful in preventing and treating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_associated_diarrhea" title="Antibiotic associated diarrhea">antibiotic associated diarrhea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Fermented milk products (such as <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>) are similarly beneficial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zinc</a> supplementation appears to be effective in both treating and preventing diarrhea among children in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiemetics">Antiemetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Antiemetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antiemetic" title="Antiemetic">Antiemetic</a> medications may be helpful for treating vomiting in children. <a href="/wiki/Ondansetron" title="Ondansetron">Ondansetron</a> has some utility, with a single dose being associated with less need for intravenous fluids, fewer hospitalizations, and decreased vomiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fedorowicz2011_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fedorowicz2011-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17279195_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17279195-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2011_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2011-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Metoclopramide" title="Metoclopramide">Metoclopramide</a> might also be helpful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cochrane2011_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cochrane2011-68">[68]</a></sup> However, the use of ondansetron might possibly be linked to an increased rate of return to hospital in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The intravenous preparation of ondansetron may be given orally if clinical judgment warrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dimenhydrinate" title="Dimenhydrinate">Dimenhydrinate</a>, while reducing vomiting, does not appear to have a significant clinical benefit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-24"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiotics">Antibiotics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Antibiotics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Antibiotics are not usually used for gastroenteritis, although they are sometimes recommended if symptoms are particularly severe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> or if a susceptible bacterial cause is isolated or suspected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> If antibiotics are to be employed, a <a href="/wiki/Macrolide" title="Macrolide">macrolide</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Azithromycin" title="Azithromycin">azithromycin</a>) is preferred over a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluoroquinolone" title="Fluoroquinolone">fluoroquinolone</a> due to higher rates of resistance to the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bact2007_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bact2007-19">[19]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudomembranous_colitis" title="Pseudomembranous colitis">Pseudomembranous colitis</a>, usually caused by antibiotic use, is managed by discontinuing the causative agent and treating it with either <a href="/wiki/Metronidazole" title="Metronidazole">metronidazole</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vancomycin" title="Vancomycin">vancomycin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandell_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mandell-73">[73]</a></sup> Bacteria and protozoans that are amenable to treatment include <i><a href="/wiki/Shigellosis" title="Shigellosis">Shigella</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonellosis" title="Salmonellosis">Salmonella typhi</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <i>Giardia</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giar2010_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Giar2010-36">[36]</a></sup> In those with <i><a href="/wiki/Giardia" title="Giardia">Giardia</a></i> species or <i>Entamoeba histolytica</i>, <a href="/wiki/Tinidazole" title="Tinidazole">tinidazole</a> treatment is recommended and superior to metronidazole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giar2010_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Giar2010-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) recommends the use of antibiotics in young children who have both bloody diarrhea and fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-25"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimotility_agents">Antimotility agents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Antimotility agents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Antimotility medication has a theoretical risk of causing complications, and although clinical experience has shown this to be unlikely,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-43">[43]</a></sup> these drugs are discouraged in people with bloody diarrhea or diarrhea that is complicated by fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Loperamide" title="Loperamide">Loperamide</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioid</a> analogue, is commonly used for the symptomatic treatment of diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SleisengerFordtran_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-SleisengerFordtran-78">[78]</a></sup> Loperamide is not recommended in children, however, as it may cross the immature blood–brain barrier and cause toxicity. <a href="/wiki/Bismuth_subsalicylate" title="Bismuth subsalicylate">Bismuth subsalicylate</a>, an insoluble complex of trivalent <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> and salicylate, can be used in mild to moderate cases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-43">[43]</a></sup> but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salicylate_toxicity" title="Salicylate toxicity">salicylate toxicity</a> is theoretically possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-26"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 19–30</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 607–1799</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/310px-Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/465px-Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/620px-Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diarrhoeal_diseases_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for diarrhea per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004 <div> <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"> <tbody><tr> <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3b3b3; color:black;"> </span> <small>no data</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65; color:black;"> </span> <small>≤less 500</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fff200; color:black;"> </span> <small>500–1000</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffdc00; color:black;"> </span> <small>1000–1500</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc600; color:black;"> </span> <small>1500–2000</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffb000; color:black;"> </span> <small>2000–2500</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a00; color:black;"> </span> <small>2500–3000</small></div> </td> <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;"> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff8400; color:black;"> </span> <small>3000–3500</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff6e00; color:black;"> </span> <small>3500–4000</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;"> </span> <small>4000–4500</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;"> </span> <small>4500–5000</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;"> </span> <small>5000–6000</small></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cb0000; color:white;"> </span> <small>≥6000</small></div> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div></div>
<p>It is estimated that there were two billion cases of gastroenteritis that resulted in 1.3 million deaths globally in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015Pre_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015Pre-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBD2015De_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-GBD2015De-7">[7]</a></sup> Children and those in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> are most commonly affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web09_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-Web09-15">[15]</a></sup> As of 2011, in those younger than five, there were about 1.7 billion cases resulting in 0.7 million deaths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walk2013_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walk2013-16">[16]</a></sup> with most of these occurring in the world's poorest nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-16"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> More than 450,000 of these fatalities are due to rotavirus in children under five years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota2012_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rota2012-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">Cholera</a> causes about three to five million cases of disease and kills approximately 100,000 people yearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cholera11_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cholera11-31">[31]</a></sup> In the developing world, children less than two years of age frequently get six or more infections a year that result in significant gastroenteritis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-17"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> It is less common in adults, partly due to the development of acquired <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980, gastroenteritis from all causes caused 4.6 million deaths in children, with the majority occurring in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mandell_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mandell-73">[73]</a></sup> Death rates were reduced significantly (to approximately 1.5 million deaths annually) by 2000, largely due to the introduction and widespread use of <a href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_therapy" title="Oral rehydration therapy">oral rehydration therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victora2000_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victora2000-80">[80]</a></sup> In the US, infections causing gastroenteritis are the second most common infection (after the <a href="/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>), and they result in between 200 and 375 million cases of acute diarrhea<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-18"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eck2011_18-12"><a href="#cite_note-Eck2011-18">[18]</a></sup> and approximately ten thousand deaths annually,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-19"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> with 150 to 300 of these deaths in children less than five years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-27"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gastroenteritis is associated with many colloquial names, including "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montezuma%27s_revenge" title="Montezuma's revenge">Montezuma's revenge</a>", "Delhi belly", "la turista", and "back door sprint", among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-20"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup> It has played a role in many military campaigns and is believed to be the origin of the term "no guts no glory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M93_17-21"><a href="#cite_note-M93-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gastroenteritis is the main reason for 3.7 million visits to physicians a year in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-28"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup> and 3 million visits in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In the United States gastroenteritis as a whole is believed to result in costs of US$23 billion per year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> with that due to rotavirus alone resulting in estimated costs of US$1 billion a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EBMED2010_1-29"><a href="#cite_note-EBMED2010-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Cholera_morbus"></span>
The first usage of "gastroenteritis" was in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Before this time it was commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a> or "cholera morbus", among others, or less specifically as "griping of the guts", "surfeit", "flux", "colic", "bowel complaint", or any one of a number of other archaic names for acute diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaic_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaic-84">[84]</a></sup> Cholera morbus is a historical term that was used to refer to gastroenteritis rather than specifically <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many of the same agents cause gastroenteritis in cats and dogs as in humans. The most common organisms are <i>Campylobacter</i>, <i>Clostridium difficile</i>, <i>Clostridium perfringens</i>, and <i>Salmonella</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A large number of toxic plants may also cause symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some agents are more specific to a certain species. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmissible_gastroenteritis_coronavirus" title="Transmissible gastroenteritis coronavirus">Transmissible gastroenteritis coronavirus</a> (TGEV) occurs in pigs resulting in vomiting, diarrhea, and dehydration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> It is believed to be introduced to pigs by wild birds and there is no specific treatment available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> It is not transmissible to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZimmermanKarriker2012_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZimmermanKarriker2012-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Enterocolitis" title="Enterocolitis">Enterocolitis</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gastroenteritis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
